201406	2003	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	92	9
201406	2004	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201406	2005	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’d like to start by echoing your welcome to Governor Fischer, Governor Brainard, and President Mester.	92	9
201406	2006	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As you said, Madam Chair, I look forward to working with all of you and learning from you.	92	9
201406	2007	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One thing I have already learned is that the name “Fisher” is surprisingly popular among central bankers.	92	9
201406	2008	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Look at our Committee.	92	9
201406	2009	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Look at the Monetary Policy Committee of the Bank of England.	92	9
201406	2010	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m tempted to say that “Fisher” is to central banking as “Williams” is to NBA basketball, but perhaps that stretches it too far.	92	9
201406	2011	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	92	9
201406	2012	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In my remarks I am going to have three themes.	92	9
201406	2013	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The first is that we haven’t seen much analysis concerning the dual-mandate benefits of the overnight RRP facility.	92	9
201406	2014	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And my own assessment, in the absence of that analysis, is that the dual-mandate benefits of the facility are likely to be small.	92	9
201406	2015	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In contrast, as some around the table have already alluded to, I think that there are very likely to be big effects on the industrial organization of short-term funding markets.	92	9
201406	2016	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s hard to predict what those effects will be, but I think it is safe to say that they might well be large.	92	9
201406	2017	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those two considerations together give rise to a sense of uneasiness for me about this decision being taken by this Committee—whether or not this decision is rightly seen as being one that is in the purview of the FOMC.	92	9
201406	2018	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m not talking in a legal sense, but more in the sense of appropriateness.	92	9
201406	2019	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn to my dual-mandate point first.	92	9
201406	2020	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This will echo some of the remarks that President Lacker made last time.	92	9
201406	2021	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In the absence of the overnight RRP facility, there will of course be gaps between short-term market interest rates and the interest rate being paid on excess reserves.	92	9
201406	2022	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As President Lacker emphasized in our previous meeting and mentioned again today, the forces of arbitrage are going to serve to keep those gaps finite and well defined.	92	9
201406	2023	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Some admittedly simple calculations by the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis staff support President Lacker’s point.	92	9
201406	2024	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those calculations suggest that a bank borrowing federal funds and investing in reserves could earn a risk-free return of more than 7 percent if that spread between the IOER rate and the fed funds rate reached 40 basis points.	92	9
201406	2025	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is taking into account things like the bank leverage ratio and the FDIC assessment fee.	92	9
201406	2026	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s worth emphasizing that the calculations suggest that the arbitrage would be even more effective if the System were to exempt bank reserves from the 4 percent floor imposed on bank leverage.	92	9
201406	2027	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I talked about a 40 basis point spread being consistent with a risk-free rate of return of 7 percent.	92	9
201406	2028	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Using the overnight RRP facility actively could close this gap further.	92	9
201406	2029	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But this is really a question about the benefits, I think.	92	9
201406	2030	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	How much would our standard measures of quadratic loss, in terms of deviations from our inflation and employment goals, rise if there were a gap of 40, 50, or 60 basis points from the IOER rate and other short-term funding rates, as opposed to the gap of, say, 20 basis points that Governor Tarullo mentioned?	92	9
201406	2031	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will repeat this, just to try to be as clear as I can be:  If we just used IOER and did not have the overnight RRP facility, I would anticipate gaps that could be as large as 40, 50, or 60 basis points emerging between the IOER rate and other short-term funding rates.	92	9
201406	2032	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	How would that magnitude of gaps affect the quadratic loss in terms of our macroeconomic objectives relative to a smaller gap of, say, 20 basis points?	92	9
201406	2033	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As far as I know, we haven’t seen any specific staff work on this point.	92	9
201406	2034	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My own assessment, in the absence of that work, is that the increase in quadratic loss would be de minimis.	92	9
201406	2035	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And this again goes back to a point that President Lacker made at our previous meeting.	92	9
201406	2036	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Making monetary policy is not about hitting a particular target for some short-term interest rate.	92	9
201406	2037	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Making monetary policy is about influencing the public’s beliefs about the evolution of financial conditions over the next few years.	92	9
201406	2038	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I believe that we can just as effectively communicate this information through changes in a single administered rate like the IOER rate as opposed to changes in the target, or target range, for a market short-term interest rate—or as opposed to changes in multiple administered rates.	92	9
201406	2039	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And this belief is supported through the experience of other central banks that use different operating procedures.	92	9
201406	2040	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So that is on the benefit side.	92	9
201406	2041	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	What about on the cost side?	92	9
201406	2042	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it’s very hard for us to know the ultimate effect of this facility on the industrial organization of short-term funding markets.	92	9
201406	2043	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And we can say we’ll start small, but I think there will be a lot of demands on the outside from other counterparties that want to have access.	92	9
201406	2044	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think the fairness criterion that the Desk has suggested using is going to force us to expand this.	92	9
201406	2045	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s really hard for us to predict how the structure of money market funds will change and what that change will mean for the funding of other financial institutions.	92	9
201406	2046	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We could use the best modeling and analysis available between now and September, but I think even with that, the answer to this question is going to be highly uncertain.	92	9
201406	2047	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So we’ve got limited dual-mandate benefits, and then big-time effects on financial markets.	92	9
201406	2048	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My last comment, then, along these lines concerns the role of the Congress and the public in making decisions about this facility.	92	9
201406	2049	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The way we operate is, we deliberate in secret without much in the way of direct oversight or input from the public.	92	9
201406	2050	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I think that form of decisionmaking is well designed and appropriately designed to prevent political considerations from influencing the course of monetary policy.	92	9
201406	2051	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Should we be using that same closed-door process to reshape the structure of the financial industry in possibly very fundamental ways— especially in the absence of being able to point to very convincing and compelling dual-mandate benefits?	92	9
201406	2052	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The Congress originally granted us the power to pay interest on reserves to depository institutions after deliberating for at least three years—probably those of you who know the history can say it was much longer than that, but I’ll say for at least three years.	92	9
201406	2053	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Should the Congress not have a say in whether we can essentially pay interest on reserves to other financial entities?	92	9
201406	2054	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	At a minimum, it would seem to me that representatives of the public should be able to provide public input on the potential effect of this potentially far-reaching decision.	92	9
201406	2055	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So those are my general comments.	92	9
201406	2056	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I see the dual-mandate benefits of this facility as small in the absence of a more detailed analysis, and I see these costs on the industry structure side as being such that it is sort of outside the purview of our usual kinds of decisionmaking in this closed-door forum.	92	9
201406	2057	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn to a couple of the questions.	92	9
201406	2058	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought that Governor Fischer’s line of comments about the fed funds rate were very apt.	92	9
201406	2059	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And to quote a Ninth District resident, “There is no need to go back to making monetary policy like it’s 1999.”	92	9
201406	2060	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	92	9
201406	2061	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s a quote from Prince, for those of you who are not tracking the Ninth District closely.	92	9
201406	2062	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	You know, we were all much younger in 1999.	92	9
201406	2063	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Everything was a lot better in 1999.	92	9
201406	2064	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But that doesn’t mean we have to make monetary policy that way.	92	9
201406	2065	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	To get serious about this, I think we can communicate effectively about the evolution of financial conditions through a single administered rate, the rate of interest on excess reserves.	92	9
201406	2066	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Governor Tarullo raised a great way of thinking about this.	92	9
201406	2067	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should just be monitoring short-term interest rates.	92	9
201406	2068	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	They are just one more indicator among a host of other indicators we can keep track of and we can be responsive to.	92	9
201406	2069	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we thought short-term interest rates were too low, for some reason, relative to what we’re trying to achieve, we could raise the rate of interest on excess reserves.	92	9
201406	2070	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Actually, Governor Stein raised this way of thinking about things with respect to long-term interest rates.	92	9
201406	2071	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is just extending his thinking to short-term interest rates.	92	9
201406	2072	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The other point I wanted to comment on has to do with reinvestment.	92	9
201406	2073	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought there was a great conversation about this.	92	9
201406	2074	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I was drawn to Vice Chairman Dudley’s language, which was more flexible, for the exit-strategy principles.	92	9
201406	2075	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you wanted a set of principles to enunciate in September, I thought Vice Chairman Dudley was right—we should be flexible in that.	92	9
201406	2076	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When it actually comes to the time of trying to communicate something more specific about the likely path of the balance sheet, I liked Governor Powell’s language, which actually might also have been Vice Chairman Dudley’s at some other point.	92	9
201406	2077	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But in any event, I thought it was a very nice idea that the change in our reinvestments will begin when the fed funds rate gets to 1 percent.	92	9
201406	2078	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	After that, I think that we should have a deterministic path for the balance sheet, under which it evolves slowly over time.	92	9
201406	2079	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So that would mean smoothing out paydowns due to reinvestments and Treasury fluctuations.	92	9
201406	2080	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We think of the balance sheet as exerting force in terms of our policy, so it makes sense for us to be thinking about a deterministic path for the balance sheet, and then using the IOER rate as our main policy tool.	92	9
201406	2081	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9

201406	2123	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	The third issue—and this is one that bothers me more generally, but it applies also in this context—is that I am much less fond of trying to fix a deterministic path for what we are going to do two or three years down the road.	92	9

201406	2127	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Well, now we are considering whether to make that the way we do monetary policy.	92	9
201406	2128	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	And if we watch what happened to the British with their forward guidance, which didn’t hold up, I don’t see that they have suffered a huge loss in credibility as of now, but that may have to do with the fact that the person who changed it wasn’t the person who said it.	92	9

201406	2146	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	It has the attraction that as we get to a much smaller size of the balance sheet, we could probably run this system operating via the banks, and we’d solve the issue of spreading the seigniorage around too widely.	92	9
201406	2147	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	In terms of the circuit breakers, you don’t want those to be so tough that you can’t act as lender of last resort in a crisis.	92	9

201406	2154	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	On the reinvestment of principal, when I wrote this the other day, I said, “By the way, what is the circumstance under which we would run off the size of the portfolio before raising the short rate?”	92	9

201406	2161	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	A final thought—if the Fed constructs this index of the federal funds rate and the Eurodollar rate, then we have really got ourselves into a case in which we’re targeting a rate which we don’t have a heck of a lot of influence over, and we’re relying on the market keeping them together.	92	9

201406	2184	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Thank you.	11	1
201406	2185	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Our exhibits are in the packet titled “Material for Staff Presentation on the Economic and Financial Situation.”	11	1
201406	2186	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Your first exhibit gives an overview of near-term domestic economic developments.	11	1
201406	2187	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As shown by the leftmost blue bar in panel 1, we have cut our estimate of first-quarter real PCE growth by nearly 1 percentage point relative to the forecast that we delivered to you in last Wednesday’s Tealbook.	11	1
201406	2188	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The downward revision is concentrated in spending on medical services, which the BEA had expected to jump as a result of the Affordable Care Act.	11	1
201406	2189	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	In the event, the first-quarter source data that became available last week actually implied a decline in medical spending, though we still expect an increase to occur sometime this year.	11	1
201406	2190	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	We expect that real PCE will accelerate over the remainder of this year, partly reflecting the anticipated step-up in medical services outlays and partly based on our read of available spending indicators—such as motor vehicle sales—and fundamentals such as wealth and income.	11	1
201406	2191	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The incoming data on housing activity, including today’s report for May, have continued to be soft.	11	1
201406	2192	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	In particular, single-family housing starts—the red line in panel 2—have changed little, on net, over the past year or so.	11	1
201406	2193	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The same is true for housing permits (the black line), which provide a better gauge of the underlying trend of construction activity than starts.	11	1
201406	2194	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	With little evidence that the housing sector has resumed its upward trajectory following last year’s sharp rise in mortgage rates, we have made noticeable downward revisions to our projection of real residential investment growth over the remainder of the near term.	11	1
201406	2195	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Turning to the business sector, panel 3 plots the latest data on orders and shipments of nondefense capital goods.	11	1
201406	2196	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The three-month moving averages of new orders (the red line) and shipments (the black line) have come in above our expectations; with orders running ahead of shipments, we continue to expect that growth in real spending on equipment and intangibles will rebound in the second quarter from its disappointing first-quarter pace.	11	1
201406	2197	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Panel 4 presents the recent data on manufacturing output, including the May reading that was released yesterday.	11	1
201406	2198	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As shown in the inset box, factory output posted a solid gain in May following swings in earlier months that we attribute to the effects of unusually severe winter weather.	11	1
201406	2199	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Smoothing through some of this monthly volatility, the chart plots the three-month moving average of manufacturing IP.	11	1
201406	2200	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As you can see, the average pace of recent gains has been about the same as what we have seen over the past few years; assuming a similar rate of increase in June, manufacturing output is on track to rise at an annual rate of 5¾ percent in the second quarter.	11	1
201406	2201	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Panel 5 summarizes our near-term outlook.	11	1
201406	2202	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	After folding in the retail sales, services, and government spending data that we received after the June Tealbook closed, we project that real GDP—line 1—will rise 4¼ percent at an annual rate in the current quarter following a 2 percent decline in the first quarter.	11	1
201406	2203	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Part of the rationale for this projected second-quarter rebound is that the first-quarter drop in GDP was led by unexpectedly steep declines in inventory investment and exports— two spending categories that carry little information for the future level of GDP.	11	1
201406	2204	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	In addition, as Jeremy Nalewaik discussed in his pre-FOMC briefing yesterday, other indicators of real activity give us reason to question the first-quarter output estimate.	11	1
201406	2205	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As another way of assessing the implications of the incoming data, the solid line in panel 6 shows forecasts for near-term real GDP growth derived from our suite of factor models.	11	1
201406	2206	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Starting from the published first-quarter figure, the model projections imply a sizable rebound in growth, with an average pace in the second and third quarters that is not markedly different from the staff forecast (the dashed line).	11	1
201406	2207	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Your next exhibit summarizes the medium-term outlook.	11	1
201406	2208	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	I will focus here on comparisons between the June Tealbook and our forecast from last December, both because December was the date of the last chart show and because the past six months have seen us significantly rethink our supply-side assumptions.	11	1
201406	2209	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As reflected in panel 1, relative to December, we have lowered 2014 and 2015 GDP growth by ½ percentage point and have lowered 2016 growth by ¼ percentage point.	11	1
201406	2210	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The factors contributing to these revisions are listed in panel 2; specifically, upward revisions to wealth and some momentum from stronger-than-expected output growth last year have added a little to projected GDP growth, while a higher exchange value of the dollar has reduced projected growth.	11	1
201406	2211	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	However, the largest cumulative revision to the medium-term outlook comes from the last item on the list—namely, the sizable reduction we have made to projected potential output growth since December.	11	1
201406	2212	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As you know, these revisions to potential reflect changes that we have made to our supply-side assumptions that allow us to better explain the joint behavior of the unemployment rate and actual output through the lens of Okun’s law.	11	1
201406	2213	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Over the forecast period, we typically pass the bulk of any potential output revision through to aggregate demand on the grounds that it will affect households’ permanent income growth and firms’ expected sales growth.	11	1
201406	2214	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	In quantitative terms, therefore, the downward revisions that we have made to projected potential GDP growth from 2014 to 2016—which are summarized in panel 3— explain the lion’s share of the reduction in our forecast for actual GDP over this period.	11	1
201406	2215	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Turning to some of the components of spending, our current expected contour for real PCE growth—shown by the gray bars in panel 4—has been revised a little lower in 2015 and 2016 in response to the downward revisions to potential.	11	1
201406	2216	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Similarly, we now have a flatter contour of business investment growth—panel 5—and largely for the same reason.	11	1
201406	2217	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	We have made a more noticeable downward revision to our outlook for residential investment relative to December (panel 6).	11	1
201406	2218	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	We still believe that the projected continued improvement in the broader economy implies that the longer-term fundamentals for the housing sector are sound.	11	1
201406	2219	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	However, the puzzling weakness we have seen in housing construction in recent quarters has led us to significantly delay our anticipated recovery in residential investment.	11	1
201406	2220	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As we discussed in the Tealbook, this round we made some small but important changes to our longer-term outlook; specifically, we reduced both longer-run potential output growth and the long-run equilibrium real federal funds rate by ¼ percentage point relative to our previous assumptions.	11	1
201406	2221	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	These changes had little net influence on the medium-term output forecast, as the boost from lower real long- maturity yields was basically offset by the effect of lower real growth prospects.	11	1
201406	2222	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Your third exhibit gives an overview of the labor market.	11	1
201406	2223	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The two employment reports that we received over the intermeeting period suggest that conditions in the labor market have been improving at a slightly faster pace than we anticipated in April.	11	1
201406	2224	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As shown in panel 1, total nonfarm payroll growth (the solid line) averaged 235,000 jobs per month over the three months ending in May, 30,000 more than we had expected.	11	1
201406	2225	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The unemployment rate—shown in the inset box—came in at 6.3 percent in both April and May, ¼ percentage point below our April Tealbook forecast.	11	1
201406	2226	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	The black line in panel 2 gives our medium-term outlook for the unemployment rate, while the red line gives our December Tealbook projection.	11	1
201406	2227	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Relative to December, the expected path of the unemployment rate is ½ percentage point lower, on average, over the 2014 to 2016 period; as we have made only small revisions to our estimate of the natural rate—the green line—over this period, the downward revision to our unemployment rate forecast mostly yields a narrower projected unemployment gap.	11	1
201406	2228	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Indeed, by the end of 2016, the unemployment rate is expected to be slightly below our estimate of the natural rate.	11	1
201406	2229	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As of May, the unemployment rate stood about 1 percentage point above our estimate of its natural rate.	11	1
201406	2230	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	While we generally view the unemployment gap as the best single indicator of labor market slack, we also examine a number of other indicators to try to assess the overall degree of labor underutilization in the economy.	11	1
201406	2231	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	For example, as panel 3 shows, the labor force participation rate remains noticeably below our estimate of its trend; moreover, there has been little net progress in closing the gap between actual and trend participation since the first half of last year.	11	1
201406	2232	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Panel 4 combines the information in the unemployment and participation rate gaps by comparing the employment-to-population ratio with the staff’s estimate of its trend, which is in turn derived from our estimates of the natural rate and trend participation.	11	1
201406	2233	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	On this basis, the employment-to-population ratio is currently about 1¼ percentage points below its trend.	11	1
201406	2234	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Another measure of underutilization—the share of those employed who are working part time for economic reasons—is plotted by the black line in panel 5.	11	1
201406	2235	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	This series has retraced less than half of its run-up over the last recession and, as a comparison with the blue line shows, remains above the level that is implied by its historical relationship with the unemployment gap.	11	1
201406	2236	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Finally, panel 6 plots three measures of wage inflation, which we would expect to increase in a tight labor market.	11	1
201406	2237	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	After declining during the recession, wage inflation appears thus far to have stayed relatively subdued.	11	1
201406	2238	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Taken as a whole, we see these and other pieces of evidence as suggesting that a significant margin of slack remains in the labor market.	11	1
201406	2239	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Your final exhibit discusses the outlook for price inflation.	11	1
201406	2240	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As shown in the table in panel 1, the data we had in hand for the June Tealbook point to second-quarter PCE inflation that is a little higher than we expected in April.	11	1
201406	2241	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	This morning we received the CPI for May, which again surprised us to the upside, with larger-than- expected increases in the food, energy, and core components.	11	1
201406	2242	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	For food, we believe that the supply disruptions that have recently boosted consumer food prices are likely to be temporary.	11	1
201406	2243	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	For core inflation, much of the surprise has been in categories from which we take little signal for future inflation.	11	1
201406	2244	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Incorporating today’s CPI release, the May PPI data we received last Friday, and recent oil price developments, we now project total PCE inflation of 2.3 percent in the current quarter and 1.9 percent next quarter; our updated forecast for core PCE inflation is 1.9 percent in the second quarter and 1.6 percent in the third quarter.	11	1
201406	2245	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Median longer-term inflation expectations from the preliminary June Michigan survey (the black line in panel 2) remain within the range seen over the past several years, while 10-year expectations from the second-quarter SPF (the blue line) were flat at 2 percent for a sixth consecutive quarter.	11	1
201406	2246	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Turning to the medium-term inflation outlook, since the December Tealbook, we have made essentially no change to our forecast for total PCE inflation (panel 3) or core inflation (panel 4) in the outyears of the projection despite a narrower projected margin of slack.	11	1
201406	2247	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	This is because we have also slightly revised down our estimate of the level of PCE inflation that would obtain in the absence of economic slack and other shocks and if the current level of longer-run inflation expectations were maintained.	11	1
201406	2248	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Specifically, for the June projection, we decided to lower our judgmental estimate of this level, which we have dubbed “underlying inflation,” from 1.9 percent to 1¾ percent.	11	1
201406	2249	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Some of the empirical evidence that informed this decision—which was reviewed at greater length in a memorandum by Deb Lindner that was sent to the Committee earlier this month—is summarized in panel 5.	11	1
201406	2250	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As can be seen from the first column of figures, point estimates of underlying core PCE inflation from the reduced-form Phillips curves that the staff uses in the judgmental forecast (lines 1 and 2) and stochastic trends from various other time-series models (lines 3 through 5) currently run from 1.3 to 1.8 percent.	11	1
201406	2251	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As the second column of figures indicates, these point estimates are not tied down with much precision.	11	1
201406	2252	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Nevertheless, we felt that a small reduction in our judgmental estimate of underlying inflation would result in a more balanced inflation forecast.	11	1
201406	2253	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	As shown in panel 6, which decomposes the determinants of our medium-term core inflation contour, because we assume that longer-run inflation expectations will remain unchanged over the medium term, we expect core inflation to move up toward this underlying pace—the blue bars—as the contribution of slack—the red bars—and other influences gradually diminishes.	11	1
201406	2254	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	After the medium term, we project that, in order to bring inflation back to its 2 percent longer-run objective, you will need to push the unemployment rate below its natural rate for several years, thereby inducing longer-run inflation expectations to edge higher.	11	1
201406	2255	2014-06-18	meeting	RUDD	Beth Anne will now continue our presentation.	11	1
201406	2256	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Not to diminish the excitement of this moment, but, as presented in the top lines in exhibit 5, the contour of our forecast for total foreign growth is almost completely unchanged from the April Tealbook.	11	1
201406	2257	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	After a relatively robust performance in the second half of last year, GDP growth turned sharply south, barely surpassing 2 percent in the first quarter.	11	1
201406	2258	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As panel 2 shows, this decline was concentrated in the emerging market economies (EMEs).	11	1
201406	2259	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	We continue to view the first-quarter drop as a pothole rather than a slippery slope and now have some evidence to back that up.	11	1
201406	2260	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As seen in the black line of panel 3, after falling precipitously in the first quarter, EME exports bounced back in April, led by China and in line with a pickup in US imports.	11	1
201406	2261	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Another source of first-quarter weakness was the high-tech sector, and recent indicators, including high-tech export orders for Taiwan (the green line), also point to recovery.	11	1
201406	2262	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In China, supportive macroeconomic policies as well as trade seem to be lifting growth this quarter, as evidenced by a turnaround in industrial production (panel 4).	11	1
201406	2263	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	We have Chinese growth (line 3 of the table) climbing further in the second half and moderating thereafter.	11	1
201406	2264	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Our baseline assumes that Chinese authorities are able to suppress financial excesses while maintaining growth near 7 percent.	11	1
201406	2265	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	But this path is far from certain.	11	1
201406	2266	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Over the past few years, as the economy has decelerated, we have wrestled with where the new normal is for China, and the weak first-quarter figure may indicate a slower underlying pace of growth than we’ve assumed.	11	1
201406	2267	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In addition, we are carefully watching the property sector, in which a sharp deceleration in house prices (panel 5) and a falloff in sales (not shown) may presage a sustained downturn in housing investment or, worse, significant financial stress.	11	1
201406	2268	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Latin America continues to disappoint.	11	1
201406	2269	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In Mexico (line 5 of the table), any nascent benefits of the government’s well-intentioned long-term structural reforms have been overshadowed by near-term tax hikes and a collapse in construction.	11	1
201406	2270	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	We assume the Mexican malaise began to lift this quarter.	11	1
201406	2271	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Indeed, consumer confidence (the red line in panel 6) has snapped back, and a surprise interest rate cut by the Bank of Mexico last week should provide support.	11	1
201406	2272	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In contrast, in Brazil, it’s still more stumble than samba.	11	1
201406	2273	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Years of overregulation, underinvestment, and inefficient government spending have left the country in desperate need of structural and fiscal reform.	11	1
201406	2274	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Consumer confidence (the orange line in panel 6) plunges on, and inflation (not shown) remains stubbornly high.	11	1
201406	2275	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	We have penciled in a modest pickup (line 6 of the table), but Brazil will need to play a beautiful game in many arenas to avoid further disappointment.	11	1
201406	2276	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Finally, as we pass the first anniversary of the “taper tantrum,” it bears noting that financial conditions (panel 7) have improved for most EMEs since the turn of the year, with credit spreads falling, capital inflows returning, and currencies (not shown) strengthening.	11	1
201406	2277	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	This is positive for EME growth, but we can’t always count on investors’ reach for yield overcoming their fear of vulnerabilities.	11	1
201406	2278	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Bouts of renewed financial stress down the road, perhaps as advanced economies normalize monetary policy, would not be surprising.	11	1
201406	2279	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	I would add that besides emerging market stresses, another risk to the outlook is the deepening conflict in Iraq.	11	1
201406	2280	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Although oil prices are up only $3 per barrel since last week, they could go much higher if the conflict is not contained.	11	1
201406	2281	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Turning to exhibit 6 and the outlook for the advanced foreign economies (AFEs), we have aggregate growth (line 1) holding at a steady and above-potential pace of 2¼ percent.	11	1
201406	2282	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	The path looks unexciting, but it may require some pretty bold monetary policies in Japan and the euro area to achieve.	11	1
201406	2283	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In Japan, central bank holdings (shown in panel 2) now amount to 50 percent of GDP and are expected to rise to nearly 75 percent by the end of 2015.	11	1
201406	2284	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As seen in line 2 of the table, smoothing through the wiggles caused by tax hikes this year and next, we expect this monetary support to help sustain Japanese growth at near 1 percent.	11	1
201406	2285	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Accommodative monetary policy is also the talk of the euro area.	11	1
201406	2286	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As Lorie discussed, the ECB took a number of steps this month to bolster recovery.	11	1
201406	2287	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	These moves were motivated by conditions that still look shaky.	11	1
201406	2288	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In particular, first-quarter GDP growth (line 3 of the table) was below 1 percent, and, as panel 3 illustrates, a number of countries posted negative growth.	11	1
201406	2289	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Moreover, bank lending (shown in panel 4) has yet to perk up.	11	1
201406	2290	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	While the unemployment rate for Germany (the blue line in panel 5) has reached historic lows, unemployment rates elsewhere remain supremely and stubbornly elevated.	11	1
201406	2291	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Divergent unemployment rates have not resulted in divergent inflation rates (shown in panel 6).	11	1
201406	2292	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Inflation for Germany has been falling alongside that of the other countries, not only contributing to overall euro-area disinflation, but also limiting the ability of the peripheral economies to gain competitiveness.	11	1
201406	2293	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Accordingly, we forecast only a gradual firming in output and a rise in inflation to 1½ percent by the end of 2016—a forecast that has been revised up just a touch on the latest ECB announcements.	11	1
201406	2294	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As shown in panel 1 of exhibit 7, we expect policy rates in both Japan and the euro area to remain near zero throughout the forecast period, whereas policy normalization is expected to start in the United Kingdom and Canada over the next year or so.	11	1
201406	2295	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Even for the United Kingdom and Canada, we expect that policy rates at the end of 2016 will be well below pre-crisis levels, despite the expectation that inflation (shown in panel 2) will be near target and output gaps (panel 3) closed.	11	1
201406	2296	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Over the next two and a half years, we still see a number of headwinds to AFE growth that depress equilibrium interest rates and help motivate relatively accommodative monetary policy.	11	1
201406	2297	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	First, as seen in panel 4, in most AFEs, fiscal policy will continue to hinder growth.	11	1
201406	2298	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Second, there remains some need for further post-crisis financial healing.	11	1
201406	2299	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In particular, corporate spreads (panel 5) are still elevated, as are household debt levels (panel 6).	11	1
201406	2300	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Finally, for most AFEs, investment (panel 7) plunged in the wake of the financial crisis and, as measured relative to pre- crisis peaks, continues to be depressed.	11	1
201406	2301	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Moreover, as discussed in your next exhibit and earlier by Lorie, markets appear to be betting that, after these headwinds fade, very low interest rates will persist.	11	1
201406	2302	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Since January, 10-year yields (panel 1) have declined across the advanced economies, driven by expectations of lower rates in the longer run—shown by the 1-year forward rates 9 years ahead in panel 2.	11	1
201406	2303	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As outlined in the bullets in panel 3, against the backdrop of rising concerns over “secular stagnation,” market participants and policymakers have speculated that the fall in long-term rates may reflect, among many factors, a reassessment of long-run potential growth in these economies and, consequently, a decline in the equilibrium interest rate that prevails in the long run.	11	1
201406	2304	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	As was raised in the Tealbook box “Changes to the Longer-Run Outlook in the United States,” we see US potential growth (the red line in panel 4) averaging 2 percent over the next decade or so, down from 3 percent between 1997 and 2005.	11	1
201406	2305	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	The staff estimates that this decline in potential growth will, all else being equal, engender a decline in the value of the real equilibrium interest rate compared with pre-crisis levels.	11	1
201406	2306	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	However, as also raised in the Tealbook box, two international factors mitigate the size of the downward revision to rates.	11	1
201406	2307	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	First, in an open economy setting, equilibrium interest rates in the United States may be affected not just by domestic potential output growth, but also by equilibrium interest rates and potential growth in the rest of the world.	11	1
201406	2308	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In contrast to the United States, total foreign potential growth (the black line in panel 4) is projected to be higher in the coming decade than in pre- crisis years, a factor that could support foreign and US interest rates in the period ahead.	11	1
201406	2309	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Interestingly, this shift up in growth occurs despite the fact that, over the next decade, potential growth in the AFEs (the blue line in panel 5) remains below its pre- crisis pace and EME potential growth is expected to steadily decline.	11	1
201406	2310	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Offsetting these declines, however, as seen in panel 6, is the fact that the share of world GDP represented by the EMEs is rising over time, increasing the weight of these faster- growing economies in total foreign potential growth.	11	1
201406	2311	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	The second factor mitigating a decline in the US equilibrium interest rate is discussed in the bullets of your last exhibit.	11	1
201406	2312	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	Before the global financial crisis, especially in the mid-2000s, countries with large current account surpluses (panel 2) were exporting their savings to advanced economies, particularly the United States, which ran current account deficits.	11	1
201406	2313	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	These flows from the “savings glut countries”—	11	1
201406	2314	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	China, Japan, other emerging Asian economies, and the oil exporters—put downward pressure on US interest rates.	11	1
201406	2315	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	For the period ahead, we anticipate that the negative pressure on US interest rates from these economies will ease from its peak immediately prior to the global financial crisis.	11	1
201406	2316	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	For starters, our projection of declining oil prices (shown in panel 3) implies that the current account surpluses of the oil exporters are likely to narrow.	11	1
201406	2317	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In addition, we anticipate that the Asian economies will rebalance some toward domestic demand, leading to an appreciation of their exchange rates (as indicated by the projected fall in the dollar against the EME currencies, the green line in panel 4) and a diminution of their current account surpluses.	11	1
201406	2318	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	An alternative simulation in the Tealbook explores the implications for US output and policy rates should EMEs promote greater rebalancing away from export- led growth.	11	1
201406	2319	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	In particular, in this scenario EME growth is assumed to be 2 percentage points higher than baseline in the next two years, driven by stronger domestic demand and with the authorities allowing their currencies to rise in response.	11	1
201406	2320	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	The higher growth and more appreciated currencies of the EMEs reduce the US net export deficit as a share of GDP (panel 5) and boost US GDP (not shown).	11	1
201406	2321	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	This leads to a federal funds rate path (the red line in panel 6) that rises more rapidly after liftoff and remains more elevated even after US GDP has returned to baseline, as higher net exports are offset by lower US domestic demand.	11	1
201406	2322	2014-06-18	meeting	WILSON	These results, although illustrative, do suggest that actions by the emerging market economies to achieve stronger and more balanced growth would raise US output and interest rates.	11	1

201406	2705	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	So there is positive momentum in the economy.	13	1

201406	2708	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	As far as negative factors are concerned, first, even before the election in Virginia, all the defense and other large lobbying firms—one of my favorites to talk to is Anheuser-Busch InBev because no congressperson will resist a beer vendor—were predicting that the Ryan–Murray budget agreement was evaporating, and all expressed fears, even before the Cantor election, that sequestration would kick back in.	13	1

201406	2715	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Looking ahead, the balance of risks, if anything favors an acceleration in GDP—and, again, respectful disagreement to my colleague from Boston, with whom I recently agreed on almost everything, much to his embarrassment.	13	1
201406	2716	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	I think that’s important because, however disappointing and uneven output growth may have been over the course of the recovery and recently, the unemployment rate has so far declined with remarkable consistency, falling by just shy of 1 percentage point per year.	13	1
201406	2717	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	With real growth prospects improving, the probability is high that, one year from now, the unemployment rate will be within the central tendency of SEP natural rate estimates.	13	1
201406	2718	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	On the inflation front, research that suggests that the unemployment rate has lost its usefulness as an indicator of near-term wage and price pressures is not compelling to me.	13	1
201406	2719	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	The modest increase that we’ve seen in wage inflation so far in this recovery is completely consistent with past experience.	13	1
201406	2720	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	We can expect significantly faster wage increases as the unemployment rate moves down.	13	1
201406	2721	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Again, we’re seeing a little microcosm of that in my District.	13	1
201406	2722	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	The trimmed mean PCE inflation measure, which President Williams kindly referred to, captures medium-term headline inflation trends, I believe, better than the conventional PCE inflation measure and responds to changes in unemployment as well as to the level of unemployment.	13	1
201406	2723	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Thanks partly to this effect, my inflation projection rises to 2 percent in 2016 and, particularly if we adopt the suggestion in Deb’s memo—I’ll come to this in just a minute— to above 2 percent thereafter.	13	1
201406	2724	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Now, I’m convinced that, in the near term, inflation responds to changes in slack as well as the level of slack.	13	1
201406	2725	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	For these reasons, I see inflation rising further over time and more quickly than does the Tealbook.	13	1
201406	2726	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	At the same time, I believe that increases in the unemployment rate are very difficult to contain once they begin.	13	1
201406	2727	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	It’s proven nearly impossible historically for policymakers to nudge the unemployment rate upward to restrain inflation.	13	1
201406	2728	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	An implication is that the risks to mis-estimating slack are asymmetric, and it’s substantially more dangerous, in our view at the Dallas Fed, to overestimate slack than to underestimate it.	13	1
201406	2729	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	I think—and I may be wrong here—the models used by the Board staff do not appear to recognize the nonlinearity in the economy’s dynamics.	13	1
201406	2730	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	I go back to the work by Neftçi in 1984.	13	1
201406	2731	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	It almost seems as though every five years after that, more work was done on this issue.	13	1
201406	2732	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Basically, he shows that the unemployment rate tends to drift downward gradually but to increase rapidly.	13	1
201406	2733	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	I conclude that one does not want to overshoot the full-employment unemployment rate willy-nilly, because after crossing that threshold it becomes nearly impossible to tap on the brakes without engineering a recession.	13	1
201406	2734	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Again, I thought Deb’s memo was excellent.	13	1
201406	2735	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	Woody Allen used to say that the most felicitous words in the English language were not “I love you” but, instead, “It is benign.”	13	1
201406	2736	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	But in the monetary lexicon, the most dangerous words I’ve read recently, David, were at the end of your memo, which says that, “in order for the Committee to achieve its 2 percent objective, a period of some overshooting of full resource utilization may be required.”	13	1
201406	2737	2014-06-18	meeting	FISHER	To me, Madam Chair, that evokes Arthur Burns.	13	1

201406	2861	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	The Carolinas, though, are seeing a pickup in loan growth and investment.	13	1
201406	2862	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	A banker in South Carolina, for example, says they’ve seen increased business optimism this year and organic loan growth reflecting expansion-related investments.	13	1
201406	2863	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	In North Carolina, bankers said that credit quality is getting better and better.	13	1
201406	2864	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	Turning to the national outlook, some changes in the Tealbook brought the staff’s forecast closer to my own projection.	13	1
201406	2865	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	The estimate of the long-run growth rate of real GDP has been lowered ¼ percentage point, the forecast for growth in residential investment this year has been reduced 3½ percentage points, and the forecast for this year’s growth in nonresidential structures has been cut 3¼ percentage points.	13	1
201406	2866	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	My own projection has not materially changed since our March submission, aside from lowering GDP growth and raising inflation in the first half as a result of the significant new data we’ve received since then.	13	1
201406	2867	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	There were times over the intermeeting period when I was tempted to make a meaningful upward revision to my projection for consumer spending, which would have pushed my GDP projection higher.	13	1
201406	2868	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	The temptation was based on the fact that consumption came in pretty strong for the fourth quarter.	13	1
201406	2869	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	It seemed on track for similar gains earlier this year despite the weather.	13	1
201406	2870	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	But recent reports on health-care expenditures and retail sales paint a different picture, making the strength in fourth-quarter consumption growth look more temporary than permanent.	13	1
201406	2871	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	So I don’t see household spending picking up much momentum in the second half of this year.	13	1
201406	2872	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	I’m also less optimistic than the Tealbook about the outlook for residential investment.	13	1
201406	2873	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	Five years on from the recession, that experience seems to have left a large segment of the population much less inclined to make major housing investments than in previous expansions.	13	1
201406	2874	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	Like President Plosser, I view this as less a cyclical phenomenon than a fairly fundamental structural shift in the housing market.	13	1
201406	2875	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	People are bound to place greater weight now on the possibility of large declines in home values and in their ability to repay.	13	1
201406	2876	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	On top of that, tightening credit availability reduces financing opportunities and provides another good reason for caution in household financial planning.	13	1
201406	2877	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	In addition, there remains a substantial overhang of homes associated with foreclosures or seriously delinquent mortgages.	13	1
201406	2878	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	So my sense is that the sluggishness in single-family starts will be fairly persistent, and that a burst of residential investment—like that in the Tealbook, albeit delayed—is relatively unlikely.	13	1
201406	2879	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	The other difference I’d highlight concerns inflation.	13	1
201406	2880	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	I really appreciated, as I said, the latest memo providing details of the staff’s thinking on inflation forecasts, but I’m not completely convinced that it will take so long for inflation to get to 2 percent.	13	1
201406	2881	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	President Williams raised some excellent points about interpreting relative price changes and the approach of relying so heavily on core for underlying inflation trends.	13	1
201406	2882	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	I think the firmer inflation readings we’ve seen of late raise for me the serious likelihood that inflation has bottomed out in this current little dip we’ve seen, and that we’re going to see reasonable progress toward our 2 percent target this year.	13	1
201406	2883	2014-06-18	meeting	LACKER	And, while the staff rightly argues against putting excessive weight on the most recent observations, surely the optimal weight is greater than zero.	13	1

201406	3040	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If that’s a general sentiment on the Committee, then I think it’s going to be very hard for us to say that long-run inflation is under our control, as we do in our longer-run goals statement.	13	1

201406	3042	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But if we’re uncomfortable exceeding maximum employment in order to move up inflation expectations, as the staff indicated is necessary to move inflation back up to 2 percent, then we can’t say that we’re going to hit 2 percent, because once inflation drifts down, we’re not willing to move it back up.	13	1
201406	3043	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s something we should talk about if that’s a general sentiment among Committee members.	13	1
201406	3044	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Okay, so the first thing is that the impact of the staff outlook is very important.	13	1
201406	3045	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The second important aspect of the Lindner memo is its implication for the behavior of inflation expectations.	13	1
201406	3046	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As you’ll hear, I took on board the estimates out of the econometrics models—the Cogley-Sargent and Stock-Watson models—more than I did the imputations from the SPF and Michigan surveys.	13	1
201406	3047	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The estimate of the trend component in the Cogley-Sargent and Stock- Watson models of inflation is currently less than 1½ percent.	13	1
201406	3048	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	What is this telling us?	13	1
201406	3049	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This means that if people in the economy form their expectations like econometricians, inflation expectations would now be materially below target.	13	1
201406	3050	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And this, I’m worrying, would, in and of itself, put further downward pressure on inflation and inflation expectations.	13	1
201406	3051	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The point on which I would differ, I guess, somewhat from the thinking of the staff is, when I look at survey evidence and financial market prices, I don’t see any evidence of that downward drift in inflation expectations at this time.	13	1
201406	3052	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My interpretation is that, unlike the models that are learning only from the data, the public is listening to us talk—to the FOMC’s communications—and we continue to be credible.	13	1
201406	3053	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Even though I agree with Governor Fischer that they don’t necessarily understand what inflation rate we’re talking about, they trust us that we’re going to hit 2 percent on the inflation rate.	13	1
201406	3054	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We continue to be credible in that the public continues to trust our willingness and ability to deliver 2 percent inflation.	13	1
201406	3055	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t think we should just count on this happy situation continuing indefinitely.	13	1

201406	3061	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Personally, I counsel against waiting for hard evidence of the unmooring of inflation expectations to spring into action on the inflation front.	13	1
201406	3062	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m sure that everyone around this table remembers that high inflation expectations had really become part of the American culture and psyche in the 1970s.	13	1

201406	3164	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	With output and unemployment gaps narrowing, I expect inflation to gradually move back to our 2 percent longer-run target over the next few years, although slightly faster than assumed in the Tealbook.	13	1

201406	3169	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In my baseline forecast, we miss on our mandates for inflation and employment on the same side.	13	1
201406	3170	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	They’re too low.	13	1
201406	3171	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	There’s no conflict or tradeoff regarding appropriate policy, at least in terms of our macro goals.	13	1
201406	3172	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	However, recent research on inflation dynamics and long-term unemployment raises the possibility of an upside risk to this forecast for inflation.	13	1
201406	3173	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	If this risk materializes, inflation could reach our target before the unemployment gap is fully closed.	13	1
201406	3174	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	And that would create a short-run tradeoff for monetary policy.	13	1
201406	3175	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’ll spend the rest of my time discussing the evidence regarding this risk and the implications for policy.	13	1
201406	3176	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Not surprisingly, much of this discussion is based on a recent paper that I wrote with Glenn Rudebusch.	13	1
201406	3177	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Signed copies are also available after dinner.	13	1
201406	3178	2014-06-18	meeting		[Laughter]	13	1
201406	3179	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	To preview, if we face this tradeoff—and I understand there are people in this room who are not convinced by this, but you think of this as a risk—it would be optimal to temporarily overshoot on inflation by a modest amount.	13	1
201406	3180	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Such an outcome balances our price-stability and maximum-employment mandates, completely consistent with the FOMC longer-run policy goals and monetary strategy statement that we agreed to back in January 2012 and have since twice reaffirmed.	13	1
201406	3181	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Various Committee participants have discussed the evidence regarding the influence of long-term unemployment versus short-term unemployment on inflation.	13	1
201406	3182	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	My own reading of the data and research leads me to conclude that it’s hard to dismiss outright the possibility that the long-term unemployed have less effect on wage and price setting than the short-term unemployed.	13	1
201406	3183	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Estimating a standard expectations-augmented Phillips curve for the period from 1960 through 2013, Glenn and I found that the short-term unemployment gap is a far more important determinant of inflation than the long-term unemployment gap.	13	1
201406	3184	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In fact, in our own model, after controlling for the short-term unemployment gap, the long-term unemployment gap provides no additional information.	13	1
201406	3185	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I recognize that some have argued that this aggregate type of analysis is sensitive to model and sample specification, and I have read those papers.	13	1
201406	3186	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	But I would mention that our findings are consistent with research by Bob Gordon, by Mark Watson, and by Alan Krueger, and they use different models and samples.	13	1
201406	3187	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Others have noted that micro studies using cross-state or cross-city variation find little differences in short- and long-term unemployment in determining inflation.	13	1
201406	3188	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think those studies are very interesting, and I’ve been following that closely.	13	1
201406	3189	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I will say that these studies are themselves not immune to some criticisms, especially around identification—basically separating causation from correlation and distinguishing between macro effects and local effects.	13	1
201406	3190	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In that regard, it is interesting to me that there is actually a very well-developed literature on this subject in the United Kingdom associated with Layard, Nickell, and Manning, which did do very careful econometrics on the British data.	13	1
201406	3191	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s a different country, but they do find results similar to those we found in the United States—that the long-term unemployed have little influence on price setting in the United Kingdom.	13	1
201406	3192	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	The way I read this evidence right now is that there’s evidence on either side, and it’s far from dispositive.	13	1
201406	3193	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	With the short-term unemployment rate close to its historical average, we should at least be open to the possibility that inflation will move up faster than expected and be prepared to respond appropriately.	13	1
201406	3194	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In other words, this is a risk to the Tealbook baseline forecast that I thought was worth investigating.	13	1
201406	3195	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	The appropriate response to such an unexpected pickup in inflation depends, of course, on whether it also implies that we’re nearing full employment.	13	1
201406	3196	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	The long-term unemployed are essentially disengaged from the labor market and beyond our policy reach.	13	1
201406	3197	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	So the short-term unemployment gap would also be a good measure of maximum employment.	13	1
201406	3198	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I want to say that the evidence does not support this view.	13	1
201406	3199	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	First, our time-series analysis in our own paper shows that the long-term unemployment share in the United States is cyclical, suggesting that, as the economy improves, the share will return to its normal level.	13	1
201406	3200	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Second, as I mentioned at previous meetings, the long-term unemployed are finding jobs, and the rate of reemployment has actually improved as the economy has gained strength.	13	1
201406	3201	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	This implies that weak aggregate demand, rather than hysteresis or other structural factors, is behind today’s elevated rate of long-term unemployment.	13	1
201406	3202	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Finally, the long-term unemployed look similar to the short-term unemployed across key characteristics such as educational attainment, occupation, and industry, especially by comparison with past recoveries.	13	1
201406	3203	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	So overall, this evidence indicates, in terms of employability, that the long-term unemployed are, in general, not that different from the short-term unemployed—other than in the duration of their joblessness.	13	1
201406	3204	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	As a consequence, the total unemployment rate, rather than the short-term unemployment rate, to my mind, remains the right metric for thinking about maximum employment in our mandate.	13	1
201406	3205	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	So combined, the evidence on inflation dynamics and the employment prospects of the long-term unemployed raise the risk that the resource gap for determining near-term inflation could, in fact, be different than the one associated with maximum employment.	13	1
201406	3206	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In other words, the limited impact of the long-term unemployed on inflation creates a wedge in the dual mandate—in particular, a divergence between the policy path that satisfies our inflation mandate and the one that satisfies our full-employment mandate.	13	1
201406	3207	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In our paper, we actually studied this using optimal policy in a simple forecasting model, and we looked at the implications of this wedge.	13	1
201406	3208	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	We find that the influence of this wedge is just like a supply shock in any other model.	13	1
201406	3209	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Appropriate policy calls for balancing the objectives of price stability and full employment by allowing inflation to temporarily overshoot its target by a modest amount.	13	1
201406	3210	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	With inflation rising more quickly, optimal policy is somewhat tighter than in the standard model that does not distinguish between short- and long-term unemployment.	13	1
201406	3211	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	So, from the point of view of the policy implications, it’s very much like a supply shock, and you can think of policy just responding to that as the inflation data confirm that that’s what’s going on.	13	1
201406	3212	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	13	1
201406	3213	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1

201406	3227	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Vice Chairman.	13	1
201406	3228	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201406	3229	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	My views, like those, I think, of most people around the table, haven’t changed much since the previous meeting.	13	1
201406	3230	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	We are getting the bounce in economic activity that we expected this quarter, and I think it looks mostly sustainable given the improvement in fundamentals, including the relatively strong payroll trend we have seen and supportive financial market conditions.	13	1
201406	3231	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	That said, though, I still have considerable uncertainty about what the growth rate is going to be during the second half of the year, after the bounce.	13	1
201406	3232	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The Tealbook estimate of 3½ percent annualized growth in the second half certainly seems doable, but I also wouldn’t be surprised if we fell back into that 2 to 2½ percent range that we have averaged since the recovery began in 2009.	13	1
201406	3233	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Such a difference in the growth rate—say, 2½ percent versus 3½ percent—I think is important because it would be meaningful in driving the expected timing of liftoff.	13	1
201406	3234	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I’d like greater clarity about that, and what the growth trend will be in the second half before making any adjustments to our communications that change expectations about the likely timing of liftoff.	13	1
201406	3235	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I also view the sustained decline in the unemployment rate as noteworthy.	13	1
201406	3236	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t think this decline tells you that much about how much slack remains in the labor market given the drop in the participation rate.	13	1
201406	3237	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	But at the same time, I don’t think we can ignore it altogether, as we don’t know how many of these discouraged workers will return to the labor force as the labor market tightens.	13	1
201406	3238	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	So, at the margin, I think the likelihood has increased that we will run out of excess slack in the labor market somewhat sooner than we had thought earlier.	13	1
201406	3239	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Another issue pertains to headwinds.	13	1
201406	3240	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	How long will they persist?	13	1
201406	3241	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	In terms of housing, I was quite skeptical about the Tealbook’s forecast at the beginning of the year.	13	1
201406	3242	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	It showed a pretty strong recovery in housing, and I continue to believe that the housing recovery will be a slow slog.	13	1
201406	3243	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	One question is, what will happen to credit availability in terms of mortgage loans?	13	1
201406	3244	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Credit scores have become a much more important factor in driving the availability of mortgage credit.	13	1
201406	3245	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	And on that point, my thinking has evolved.	13	1
201406	3246	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Earlier, I thought that credit availability would improve relatively quickly as the financial sector returned to health and housing prices recovered.	13	1
201406	3247	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	But now I think that this bifurcation across FICO scores will turn out to be much more persistent.	13	1
201406	3248	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The reason for that is, I think, that the mortgage-lending decision fundamentally changed.	13	1
201406	3249	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Prior to the crisis, the security of the loan rested primarily on the house as collateral.	13	1
201406	3250	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The credit quality of the borrower was viewed as much less important, and we certainly saw that in terms of the type of people that got mortgage credit during the crisis.	13	1
201406	3251	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	This presumably made sense in a world in which housing prices typically rose, because that drove down the loan-to-value ratio and drove up the margin of protection to the lender.	13	1
201406	3252	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	But I think the crisis changed all of this.	13	1
201406	3253	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Housing prices declined significantly, and people found out that the foreclosure process was painfully slow.	13	1
201406	3254	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Thus, the collateral of the house offered much less protection to the lender than had been anticipated at the time the loan was made—hence, the need to place a greater weight on the creditworthiness of the borrower.	13	1
201406	3255	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t think this is something that is going to change anytime soon.	13	1
201406	3256	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, when you put this together with the increased student loan debt burdens and higher down payment requirements, this suggests that the housing headwinds will be with us for a long time.	13	1
201406	3257	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, I’d like to pose and answer a few questions about the low level of volatility that we have seen in currency, equity, and fixed-income markets.	13	1
201406	3258	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	This does cause me some concern that market participants will respond to this by taking on greater risk.	13	1
201406	3259	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	That would prove problematic when volatility inevitably increases.	13	1
201406	3260	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The first question is, why is volatility so low?	13	1
201406	3261	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	As I see it, there are two broad sets of factors.	13	1
201406	3262	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	First, the economy is evolving in a way close to the consensus outlook, so existing positioning doesn’t need to be adjusted because the outlook hasn’t changed in a material way.	13	1
201406	3263	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	And, second, I think there is remarkably little disagreement about the outlook; the dispersion of the forecast is quite narrow.	13	1
201406	3264	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The second question is, are we responsible for this low level of volatility?	13	1
201406	3265	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Well, I think we are partially responsible.	13	1
201406	3266	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Our gradual, predictable taper of our asset purchases and our forward guidance with respect to liftoff does help reduce uncertainty about the path of short-term rates, and this contributes to less volatility.	13	1
201406	3267	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	That raises the question, should we do something about this if we are partly responsible for this low volatility?	13	1
201406	3268	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Well, I do think we should remind investors that the world is uncertain, and that this low volatility will inevitably end at some point in the future.	13	1
201406	3269	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	We want investors to behave in a way consistent with the risks that exist and how the world might unfold in the future.	13	1
201406	3270	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	But I don’t think we want to deliberately create more uncertainty about monetary policy just in order to make financial markets more volatile.	13	1
201406	3271	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	That is because low volatility also contributes to rising stock prices and lower term premiums that make financial conditions more accommodative, and that is actually a goal of policy.	13	1
201406	3272	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	At the zero lower bound, keeping financial conditions accommodative is what we’re trying to achieve.	13	1
201406	3273	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	As I see it, we’re essentially incurring some increased risk on financial stability—in other words, we are generating lower volatility—in order to generate easier financial conditions and faster progress to our dual mandate objectives.	13	1
201406	3274	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	For myself, I think the tradeoff here is still favorable.	13	1
201406	3275	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Although there is increased risk- taking, leverage hasn’t increased much and credit growth is sluggish.	13	1
201406	3276	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I think the impact of a sharp rise in volatility is manageable.	13	1
201406	3277	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	In contrast, if we weren’t at the zero lower bound, I would feel differently.	13	1
201406	3278	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Then, it wouldn’t be such an attractive tradeoff.	13	1
201406	3279	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	We could have more volatility and less financial-stability risk, but we could offset that impact on financial conditions by lowering short-term interest rates.	13	1
201406	3280	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	In the current setup, this isn’t available to us.	13	1
201406	3281	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The bottom line for me is, at the zero lower bound, you may want to damp volatility to make financial conditions easier, even though there might be some payback for this later.	13	1
201406	3282	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you.	13	1

201406	3302	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	We have some key questions now.	13	1

201406	3311	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Well, it is not going to become very expansionary, but we may get a little bit out of state and local governments if the economy begins to expand more rapidly, as is the forecast.	13	1

201406	3330	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Vice Chairman Dudley just talked very convincingly about how to think about low volatility in the context of, nonetheless, having high uncertainty about the future.	13	1
201406	3331	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	But I want to quote from the Tealbook.	13	1
201406	3332	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Apropos the real federal funds rate, the Tealbook emphasizes—this is on page 31 of Tealbook A—“the high degree of uncertainty around these estimates.”	13	1
201406	3333	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	First, of the long-run real federal funds rate, “70 percent confidence interval around our estimate of 1¾ percent would run from negative ¾ percent to 4¼ percent.”	13	1
201406	3334	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	I don’t know how to think in that context of changes of ¼ percent that are possible, and so forth.	13	1
201406	3335	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Now, trend output growth can be estimated more precisely with a standard error of just over ½ percentage point.	13	1
201406	3336	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	But we should recognize, if I can quote Herb Stein, which I will do frequently in the years to come, the difference between a growth rate of 1 percent and a growth rate of 2 percent is 100 percent.	13	1
201406	3337	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	And the difference between a rate of per capita growth of 1¼ percent and 1 percent, which is what we have changed to, is 20 percent.	13	1
201406	3338	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	That is not small.	13	1

201406	3341	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	That’s a big change, and I also don’t know what we should do about it.	13	1
201406	3342	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	We had a little bit of a discussion of this yesterday—I don’t remember in which context, but we need to think about that.	13	1

201406	3345	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	I don’t believe that the rates of uncertainty we have in the economy now are representative of the behavior of the economy, and the rates of uncertainty reflected in the VIX or something like that, are in fact the amount of uncertainty that is around.	13	1

201406	3349	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	I think we’ve got to not use uncertainty as an excuse for not achieving what we may want to achieve, but just to say, “These are our best estimates, but there is a lot of uncertainty out there in the world.	13	1
201406	3350	2014-06-18	meeting	FISCHER	Many things can happen, and we will make responses to changes in underlying conditions, and you should expect that.	13	1

201406	3357	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201406	3358	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	A few quick points, some of which are connected.	13	1
201406	3359	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	First, I subscribe to the general view many of you have already stated that the significantly increased momentum of this quarter will continue through the year, though probably at a somewhat reduced pace.	13	1
