201406	3361	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	And from what I’ve read, some of that is just going to come due.	13	1
201406	3362	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Second, though—and this is a variation on the theme sounded by Vice Chairman Dudley and Governor Fischer over the past few minutes—I find it a little hard to totally ignore Q1.	13	1
201406	3363	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I mean, subject to data revisions that would make things look a little less bad, the early part of the year wasn’t a hiccup; it was more like coughing up blood.	13	1
201406	3364	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	And since weather is, at most, an incomplete explanation, as Governor Fischer just pointed out, particularly for the housing slowdown, I think there is probably at least a little bit of reason for lingering concern as to whether there is some weakness underlying the recent bounceback.	13	1
201406	3365	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I think that’s connected to some of the things Bill was saying.	13	1
201406	3366	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Third, there certainly continues to be improvement in the labor market.	13	1
201406	3367	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Everybody cited it.	13	1
201406	3368	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	By definition, that means there must be less slack than there was six months ago.	13	1
201406	3369	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	But I think it’s still hard to say just how much less slack there is.	13	1
201406	3370	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Some of the incoming data, I think, can still be read in ways that are consistent with both of the narratives we have been hearing over the past year or two or three—for example, the recent uptick in job openings has been read by some as an indication of latent mismatch problems now manifesting themselves, suggesting that there is less slack.	13	1
201406	3371	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	But I think you could also read it as an indication that employers are finally moving from talking about more hiring toward taking the steps to actually do more hiring.	13	1
201406	3372	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	What will ensue will be more hiring and perhaps increased employment of the long-term unemployed, whose rates of job-taking, as Stan just noted, have picked up some.	13	1
201406	3373	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Similarly, I think the relative impact of the long-term unemployed on wages and inflation is going to continue to be hard to pin down.	13	1
201406	3374	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I will confess to not having read the new paper coauthored by President Williams.	13	1
201406	3375	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I didn’t download it and read it last night, President Williams—I’m sorry—but I will.	13	1
201406	3376	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	As he was describing it yesterday, I was reminded again that until this recession and recovery, short-term and long-term unemployment in the United States had essentially moved together for more than half a century.	13	1
201406	3377	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	In some sense, the current situation is really the only observation we have to make judgments about the implications of long-term unemployment in a context in which it isn’t changing, more or less, in lockstep with short-term unemployment.	13	1
201406	3378	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	That is why I think we are going to continue to see good, thoughtful work on both sides of this issue for a while to come, and why, as I said a couple of meetings ago, I think we are just going to have to be pragmatic in making decisions.	13	1
201406	3379	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Fourth, we have definitely seen some early signs of a pickup in wage increases, though not so much in total compensation.	13	1
201406	3380	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I believe we should all hope that this trend continues and, indeed, accelerates, since PCE increases are going to be hard to sustain at the levels that are anticipated in the Tealbook in the absence of significant increases in household income.	13	1
201406	3381	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I mean, we are not going to be able to continue to rely on wealth effects from the asset appreciation over the past year or so and the declining saving rates that we have seen over the past year or so.	13	1
201406	3382	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	Fifth, and finally, unless there is something ominous lurking in the Q1 experience, it does seem as though the biggest downside risks to the US economy are foreign in origin, both economic and geopolitical.	13	1
201406	3383	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	On the economic side, people have mentioned—Beth Anne did yesterday—the case of possible Chinese inability to maintain growth above 7 percent.	13	1
201406	3384	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	I have also begun to wonder some about Japan, which seems to be slipping from the relatively favorable trajectory that it established last year.	13	1
201406	3385	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	On the geopolitical front—this is all pretty obvious—there has been some reduction in risk of broad-based fallout from Ukraine, but it’s still material.	13	1
201406	3386	2014-06-18	meeting	TARULLO	As a couple of people noted yesterday, Iraq has returned to the fore as a source of potentially substantial instability with rising effects of the type we have already seen on oil markets and beyond.	13	1

201406	3389	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Powell.	13	1
201406	3390	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201406	3391	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	My forecast is close to that of the Tealbook baseline, and I continue to see a good bit of slack in the economy and also the absence of strong evidence of tightness either in prices or wages or other metrics.	13	1
201406	3392	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	I’d like to focus for a few minutes on conditions in the financial markets, which I see as a potential risk to this forecast.	13	1
201406	3393	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	After almost six years of highly-accommodative policy, the risks are out there and continue to build.	13	1
201406	3394	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	To be specific, the risk in the near term, to me, is not that of a meltdown at the core of the system, which is much stronger and better protected than before the crisis, but rather that of a sharp correction amplified by the liquidity mismatch in the markets that would damage or halt the progress of what is still a weak economy.	13	1
201406	3395	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	As we’ve discussed, and I would be in agreement generally with Bill’s approach to this question, but as we discussed yesterday morning with the staff, implied volatility for equities, currencies, and long- and short-term fixed income are at historically low levels around the globe and market participants have pointed to a number of explanations for that.	13	1
201406	3396	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Particularly important for me would be, along with low realized volatility and the reach for yield, the guidance that this Committee and other central banks have given, that rates will remain low for the foreseeable future.	13	1
201406	3397	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	This explanation, to me, cuts across and supports all of the other explanations.	13	1
201406	3398	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Low volatility gives a feeling of safety and encourages the taking of risks, often in the form of levered bets, and a sudden increase in volatility can also lead to a sudden flight from these positions, big price movements across financial asset classes, and a significant tightening of financial conditions.	13	1
201406	3399	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	The reach for yield is also evident across fixed-income markets—for example, there has been a resumption of strong flows into emerging market bond funds, which are often regarded as the canary in the coal mine.	13	1
201406	3400	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Investors have also shown strong appetite for European spread products, including peripheral, sovereign, and bank debt.	13	1
201406	3401	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Conditions in the leveraged finance markets in the United States and Europe are extremely loose.	13	1
201406	3402	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	In the United States, leverage is high, although not quite at all-time, pre-crisis highs.	13	1
201406	3403	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Yield structures are historically weak with covenant-lite loans at an all-time high and also with less visible and more complex terms that have typically precluded, for example, the incurrence of additional debt and payments to equity holders.	13	1
201406	3404	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	The share of issuance from low- rated borrowers—single-B and triple-C—is at an all-time high.	13	1
201406	3405	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Rates and spreads are also at or near historic lows.	13	1
201406	3406	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	With the support of the economic environment, low rates, and no covenants to violate, defaults are low and should remain low for a while, but these trends suggest that the ultimate wave of defaults will be quite large when it does materialize.	13	1
201406	3407	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	As President Fisher raised yesterday, wholesale investors are piling into newly created CLOs, and dealers are offering them leverage to fund their investments.	13	1
201406	3408	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	In this case, the investor’s position is in a leveraged investment vehicle that funds leverage at the level of the corporate buyer.	13	1
201406	3409	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	From an investor’s standpoint, this is three levels of leverage.	13	1
201406	3410	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	The point here is not to suggest that these are at all like the systemically dangerous SIVs of 2007, but more to illustrate the pressure that investors are under to find yield and the risk that they are taking.	13	1
201406	3411	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Meanwhile, compared with the pre-crisis era, availability and provision of liquidity in the markets is less certain and more at risk of drying up exactly when it’s needed.	13	1
201406	3412	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Dealer fixed- income inventories remain well below pre-crisis levels despite huge inflows into this sector since the crisis, much of it from retail investors, and many attribute this to the changed regulatory environment of the Volcker rule and the leverage ratio and stronger supervisory guidance about balance sheet risks.	13	1
201406	3413	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	ETFs and other retail-oriented investment vehicles own an increasing share of less liquid fixed-income products, exposing potentially unsuspecting retail investors to large liquidity risks.	13	1
201406	3414	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Putting these factors together, there’s widespread concern that a return to higher levels of volatility and a material repricing of these assets, perhaps triggered by a central bank decision to begin raising rates, would be amplified by strong retail outflows and low liquidity.	13	1
201406	3415	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	The result would be a sudden tightening in financial conditions and material real economy effects.	13	1
201406	3416	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	So what to do about it?	13	1
201406	3417	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	In my view at this point, this is not particularly an argument for bringing forward rate increases, nor are there obvious regulatory tools close to hand—for example, there’s a growing amount of anecdotal information that suggests that hedge funds and private-equity firms are busily building their own platforms to initiate and underwrite leverage financings as new regulations and our guidance put more pressure on regulated institutions.	13	1
201406	3418	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	It seems to me to be a task mainly for communication, on which a great deal rides.	13	1
201406	3419	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	I must say I found the statement by Governor Carney of the Bank of England last week interesting in this regard.	13	1
201406	3420	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	As we all know, he noted that the bank’s first rate hike could happen sooner than markets currently expect, and this unexpected statement seems to have been intended to produce a reaction, which it did.	13	1
201406	3421	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	Sterling rates and volatility moved up significantly.	13	1
201406	3422	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	After a short delay, US rates and volatility also moved up in a more muted fashion as investors digested this speech, as the US is seen as being only a few months behind the Bank of England in tightening.	13	1
201406	3423	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	The Carney speech shows the sensitivity of the markets to the slightest hint of an actual rate increase, even one that is thought likely to be at least a few months off.	13	1
201406	3424	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	It also shows one possible way to avoid a disconnect between market expectations and Committee intentions.	13	1
201406	3425	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	I’m not recommending this for today, I’m just pointing out that it is important that we avoid such a disconnect.	13	1
201406	3426	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	In my view, in the short term, Governor Carney has done us a small favor by shaking the market out of its complacency, and I just will close by saying that this issue will require close watching and perhaps eventually action along these lines.	13	1

201406	3444	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Indeed, conceivably, the decline is related to the structural phenomenon that President Fisher mentioned yesterday, namely, the disappearance of middle- class jobs.	13	1
201406	3445	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	I think the evidence does suggest that a disproportionate share of job loss during the recession came in middle-paying occupations, whereas the gains since then have been tilted somewhat toward lower-paying occupations.	13	1
201406	3446	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	On the topic of how far we remain from our maximum employment goal, this is something we will surely be debating in the coming months.	13	1

201406	3448	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	This reflects the fact that an unusually large share of the work force is involuntarily underemployed, as well as the unusually large decline we’ve seen in labor force participation, a portion of which arguably reflects cyclical factors.	13	1
201406	3449	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	These forms of shadow underemployment or unemployment are disproportionately high relative to U-3.	13	1

201406	3458	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Moreover, the ability of firms to replace workers with labor-saving technology—and they’re willing to do so if they encounter wage pressures, a phenomenon President Fisher described in his remarks—could be a further factor holding wage growth down.	13	1
201406	3459	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	In fact, I personally wonder if such technological possibilities may be contributing to the tendency we’ve seen for quite a number of years now for real wage growth to lag productivity growth.	13	1

201406	3461	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	If the markup and the share of labor income in GDP do not stabilize, and if the marginal propensity to consume out of labor income exceeds that from capital income, we could easily see growth remaining stuck near 2 percent rather than gradually strengthening as in the Tealbook forecast.	13	1

201406	3497	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	With respect to the outlook, I haven’t changed my baseline forecast very much at all since our previous meeting, and I, too, see the broad range of incoming evidence as suggesting continued improvement in the labor market.	13	1
201406	3498	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	That said, I agree with those who see the decline in U-3 as overstating that degree of improvement, and I particularly see a portion of the decline in the participation rate as reflecting discouragement.	13	1
201406	3499	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	With respect to the prospects for growth and continued improvement in the labor market, I frankly found recent data to be quite perplexing.	13	1
201406	3500	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	At this stage, it looks like we’ll see a pretty alarming decline in first-quarter GDP, and although most of the decline is due to large negative contributions from net exports and inventories, private domestic final purchases also seem to have slowed, and the most recent retail sales numbers haven’t been all that encouraging either.	13	1
201406	3501	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	As Governor Fischer mentioned, it’s not clear we should take heart from the fact that net exports and inventories declined either.	13	1
201406	3502	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	There are real crosscurrents here.	13	1
201406	3503	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	As I mentioned, I am also worried by the continued failure of real wages to grow in line with productivity.	13	1
201406	3504	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	I agree with Tealbook that as the labor market tightens this trend will probably change, but I do see it as a downside risk to the outlook for consumer spending.	13	1
201406	3505	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Without denying the substantial progress the economy has made toward returning to maximum employment, these observations underscore to me the importance of remaining cautious as we approach the question of the appropriate timing for removing accommodation.	13	1
201406	3506	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	And I would also just emphasize that I, too, am concerned about developments in financial markets and the signs we may be seeing of complacency in some segments.	13	1
201406	3507	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	In addition to continued general vigilance in monitoring, I think applying appropriate supervisory and regulatory measures is in order.	13	1
201406	3508	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	I also do think it’s important to lean against any tendency that develops in markets to view the FOMC as making any unconditional policy promises.	13	1
201406	3509	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	In other words, I think we should emphasize—and this is something that Governor Fischer emphasized, and Governor Powell and Vice Chairman Dudley also commented on—that policy is truly data-dependent.	13	1
201406	3510	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	If, based on our reading of labor market conditions, inflation, and financial conditions, the outlook were to strengthen materially, policy accommodation would be removed sooner than markets might anticipate, and market participants need to be aware of this possibility.	13	1
201406	3511	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	However, I do agree with Vice Chairman Dudley that I would be reluctant to purposely say something to try to jolt expectations and to purposely raise volatility, given that we’re against the zero lower bound, and that’s a delicate balance, but I think it’s one we need to navigate.	13	1

201406	3675	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, let me turn to a policy change that I believe we should take up over the next two meetings.	23	2
201406	3676	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I suggested in the previous go-round, I am highly concerned that our current approach to policy could lead to unanchored inflation expectations.	23	2
201406	3677	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Our policy is focused on reducing accommodation, and it systematically communicates to the public that the FOMC is either unwilling or unable to influence the course of inflation over the next 5 to 10 years.	23	2
201406	3678	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This message is detrimental to the promotion of price stability and our credibility, more generally.	23	2
201406	3679	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I believe that we should be planning for the possibility of more-aggressive responses to low inflation.	23	2
201406	3680	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One such response is contained in the language of alternative A, which says that we will keep interest rates extraordinarily low at least until the one- to two-year-ahead outlook for inflation rises back to 2 percent.	23	2
201406	3681	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That is in paragraph 5 of alternative A.	23	2

201406	3683	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, given the lessons from the Bank of Japan that I mentioned in the prior go-round, I actually don’t think this language goes far enough.	23	2
201406	3684	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	With this language, the FOMC sets no real deadline for returning inflation back to 2 percent, and so it continues to suggest that it sees persistently low inflation as part of a new normal.	23	2
201406	3685	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is what I think would be useful to work on over the next couple of meetings:  How can we build deadlines into our response to persistently low inflation?	23	2

201406	3688	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Put another way, how can we systematically commit to providing more accommodation if inflation and the outlook for inflation does not return back to 2 percent by, say—and I’m naming a date out of nowhere—mid-2016?	23	2

201406	3692	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These are only a couple of alternatives, and I’m sure there are other approaches that would be useful.	23	2

201406	3702	2014-06-18	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I think that, as a Committee, it’s worth rethinking about what that would mean, but right now our communication is PCE inflation is going to be at 2 percent.	23	2

201406	3809	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I’m still of the view that this is not a good approach to policy or to our communications.	23	2
201406	3810	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	If the size of our balance sheet has the effects we’ve long asserted, and it’s providing accommodation as we claim, then  maintaining a large balance sheet during the exit will require us to raise rates more and higher than we otherwise would to achieve any given stance of monetary policy.	23	2
201406	3811	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	The balance sheet and interest rate policy are intertwined.	23	2
201406	3812	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	To argue otherwise undermines the credibility of our earlier messages for balance sheet policy.	23	2
201406	3813	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	Why do we want to insist on an exit strategy that raises short-term rates higher and faster simply because we do not wish to sell assets?	23	2
201406	3814	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I have yet to see a careful analysis of this tradeoff and why selling wouldn’t be perhaps the preferable approach.	23	2
201406	3815	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	Now, there could be a story in which there are differential effects on the economy of a higher future short-term rate versus the effects on term premiums or other things that people have argued about the balance sheet, but again, I haven’t seen an analysis of those challenges and the tradeoffs that are involved, and I think we need to think about that.	23	2
201406	3816	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	One of the arguments that people have, I think, is that we wanted to make sure that we didn’t sell MBS because we were afraid about the housing market—that is, we wanted to make sure we continued our credit allocation policies toward housing.	23	2
201406	3817	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	But, of course, what that means, if that’s the way this works, is that having to raise other short-term rates faster and higher in order to accomplish the not-selling of assets and the provision of credit allocation to housing, means we are imposing higher costs on other borrowers.	23	2
201406	3818	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	Why is that desirable?	23	2
201406	3819	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	Why do we want to pursue that policy?	23	2
201406	3820	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I have just yet to hear a coherent case that justifies that sort of action.	23	2
201406	3821	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I think those are some things we need to work on and need to think about both in our language and in our statements and our policy choices as we go forward.	23	2
201406	3822	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	But for today, I can support alternative B as written.	23	2
201406	3823	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I have no strong objections to President Kocherlakota’s suggestion of adding in that statement from alternative A, although I might have a small bias to sort of say let’s take a minimalist approach at this meeting.	23	2
201406	3824	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	But I do think that the suggestion has some merit because it reestablishes that the Committee is not just worried about inflation being low.	23	2
201406	3825	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	We actually believe that inflation is going to get to our target.	23	2
201406	3826	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	I think there’s some merit to that.	23	2
201406	3827	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	But I’m not convinced that now is the right meeting to put that in, but I think in the future it would be worth considering.	23	2
201406	3828	2014-06-18	meeting	PLOSSER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2

201406	3851	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	I think taking that buffer step out of the exit process is going to make for a much rockier ride as we get close to the date of policy liftoff.	23	2
201406	3852	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Let me just elaborate on these points a little bit.	23	2
201406	3853	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	First of all, I think current policy is not sufficiently state-contingent.	23	2
201406	3854	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	It’s too driven by calendar considerations.	23	2
201406	3855	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Some of you may recall that last year at this time Chairman Bernanke had a press conference.	23	2
201406	3856	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	It was a raucous one.	23	2
201406	3857	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	He said at that press conference that unemployment would be 7 percent by June 2014, and that QE would be ended.	23	2
201406	3858	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	We are amazingly far ahead of this schedule on unemployment—we’re at 6.3 percent—and far behind on policy adjustment.	23	2
201406	3859	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	We haven’t brought the QE program to a close as of this point.	23	2
201406	3860	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	With most forecasts predicting rapid growth in the current quarter and through the rest of the year, we may have to consider altering plans and deviating from our preset pace.	23	2
201406	3861	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Calendar- based policy has a high risk of getting out of sync with actual economic developments.	23	2

201406	3863	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	We saw yesterday that, by far, the largest component of the staff inflation forecast is a constant, representing longer-term inflation expectations.	23	2
201406	3864	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	I do not think it is reasonable to interpret longer-term inflation expectations as drifting down at this juncture since, for instance, the SPF expectations of core PCE inflation remain at 2 percent, and other measures are also stable.	23	2
201406	3865	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Accordingly, I think everyone should add 25 basis points to the staff forecast for inflation, which then puts inflation at target in the medium term.	23	2

201406	3867	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	If inflation expectations are declining, then that is a very serious issue, indeed, for the Committee, and I agree completely with President Kocherlakota on this.	23	2
201406	3868	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	I have advocated this in the past, that we definitely need to defend our inflation target from below.	23	2

201406	3871	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Also, I do not agree that if longer-term inflation expectations are drifting down that that’s a problem that can be fixed by cyclical adjustment.	23	2
201406	3872	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	The way I would think about models is that you’ve got those inflation expectations more or less fixed, and that the economy is going to cycle around those expectations, and it’s going to be very hard to move away from them.	23	2
201406	3873	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	Even if inflation goes above the longer-term expectations, markets would be interpreting that as a cyclical factor, not as a structural factor.	23	2

201406	3875	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	But I—again, my view is that we are centered on our goal, and it would take more convincing to get me to move off that view in the near term.	23	2

201406	3896	2014-06-18	meeting	BULLARD	One of the things we have been concerned about is, as we get to the end of the QE program, all of the talk is going to be about, well, are they going to raise rates at the next meeting, and so on.	23	2

201406	3966	2014-06-18	meeting	EVANS	Our first priority should be to exit the zero lower bound organically, as quickly as possible.	23	2

201406	4000	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I would like to take this opportunity to highlight an additional challenge relating to the global monetary policy environment.	23	2
201406	4001	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	I recently returned from visits to four foreign countries: South Korea, Japan, Germany, and Texas.	23	2
201406	4002	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	23	2
201406	4003	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	And my visits to Japan and Germany were just an apt reminder of the divergent paths that the major central banks of the world are taking in terms of monetary policy currently and over the next couple of years.	23	2
201406	4004	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Both the Bank of Japan and the ECB are in the early stages of sizable and prolonged expansions of monetary accommodation.	23	2
201406	4005	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	My own view is that these steps are entirely appropriate, and I am encouraged by the signs of progress in escaping deflation in Japan.	23	2
201406	4006	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	However, I foresee a tug of war in global financial markets as we and the Bank of England withdraw monetary accommodation at the same time that the ECB and the Bank of Japan are adding massively to accommodation.	23	2
201406	4007	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	As research on the effects of QE has shown, policy actions in one major economy spill over to the financial conditions, especially interest rates and exchange rates, in other countries.	23	2
201406	4008	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	This came up in the primary dealer survey as one of the factors—the policy actions of other countries—that explain why interest rates have been coming down globally, and we know this.	23	2
201406	4009	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	But this divergence in the policy direction across the globe will add an additional dimension that may complicate our task of achieving the appropriate set of financial conditions supportive of our employment and price stability goals in the period ahead.	23	2
201406	4010	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, it may exacerbate carry trade, reach-for-yield, and short-term capital flows with financial-stability implications.	23	2
201406	4011	2014-06-18	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I mention this issue not with some goal of advocating a particular policy, but, rather, to highlight this issue as one we should have on our radar screen and one we should be including and accounting for in our analysis involving future financial conditions.	23	2

201406	4089	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	As a result of all that, working with the Board staff, I’ve started to look at earlier tightening cycles to see what light they might shed, and market participants and commentators are out there doing exactly the same thing.	23	2
201406	4090	2014-06-18	meeting	POWELL	For example, I met with Board staff and also with a hedge fund a couple of weeks ago that had looked at the previous seven policy normalization cycles and found a clear pattern under which the increase in long-term rates in a tightening cycle has increasingly taken place before the FOMC’s first actual rate hike, which I think sheds some light and helps make some sense on last summer’s taper tantrum.	23	2

201406	4138	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	If policy were as easy as implied by the current level of real short-term rates relative to the 1¾ percent long-run equilibrium real rate, then the economy should be growing much faster than the trajectory we’ve actually been on.	23	2
201406	4139	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Another issue worth considering with respect to these Taylor rule calculations, if the unemployment rate is artificially or temporarily depressed because of a drop in the participation rate, and if the Taylor rule’s measure of the output gap is based on the unemployment rate, which seems to be the way the staff does it, then it would seem that the Taylor rule formulations understate the output gap because they’re taking on board fully the drop in the unemployment rate, which has been driven in part by the participation rate.	23	2
201406	4140	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s another issue, I think.	23	2
201406	4141	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	The bottom line for me is, I think I’ve said this over and over again, but I’m going to say it one more time.	23	2
201406	4142	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Be careful not to let these Taylor rule calculations influence your assessment of the appropriate stance of monetary policy.	23	2
201406	4143	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, we’re obviously not doing that because the Committee supports alternative B and it’s not following the Taylor rule formulation, but I do think these rules are a bit misleading in the current set of circumstances.	23	2
201406	4144	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, let me just talk very, very briefly on reinvestment.	23	2
201406	4145	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I’m not going to go through all the arguments I made yesterday, but just state two things.	23	2
201406	4146	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	One, the reason why I want to end reinvestment after rather than before liftoff is very simple.	23	2
201406	4147	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Our priority should be to get off the zero lower bound as soon as possible, consistent with the dual-mandate objectives.	23	2
201406	4148	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	While it’s true that, on a model-based basis, ending reinvestment first wouldn’t supposedly do anything to short- and long-term rates, or be very minor, we did an experiment last summer that was supposedly very similar, in terms of the taper tantrum, and we saw not only a very large rise in long-term rates, but we also saw a very significant change in the path of short-term rate expectations.	23	2
201406	4149	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t think you can model these things.	23	2
201406	4150	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	I think you just see how markets can sometimes react in a very violent way, and so I don’t want to take that risk because if we were to experience that sort of event, we might not be able to get off the zero lower bound as soon as we would if we reversed the order and did the reinvestment later.	23	2
201406	4151	2014-06-18	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201406	4152	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2

201406	4154	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	As we went around, I think the vast majority of you preferred October and to have me do this in the press conference if it comes up.	23	2
201406	4155	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	I did hear some interest in putting it in the statement, but as I understood it, you were willing to go along with the press conference.	23	2
201406	4156	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	I do agree that there could be some reaction to this, and I will do my very best to minimize it, but I don’t want to downplay the potential for some reaction.	23	2
201406	4157	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	It’s because I expect that that I don’t want to see it in the statement, but I think that’s real.	23	2
201406	4158	2014-06-18	meeting	YELLEN	With respect to alternative B, there were a couple of proposals for changes, but I think overwhelmingly in the go-round, what I heard was support to basically leave alternative B as it is with minimal red ink today.	23	2
